<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Greenhouse gas</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><div class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile" id="mw-fr-revisiontag"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."/><img alt="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;"/></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Greenhouse_gas">reviewed</a> on <i>1 November 2018</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Effect.svg"><img alt="refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="577" data-file-width="750" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Greenhouse_Effect.svg/350px-Greenhouse_Effect.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Greenhouse_Effect.svg/525px-Greenhouse_Effect.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Greenhouse_Effect.svg/700px-Greenhouse_Effect.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Effect.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">Greenhouse effect</a> schematic showing <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> flows between <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere</a>, and Earth's <a href="/wiki/Earth#Surface" title="Earth">surface</a>. <a href="/wiki/Irradiance" title="Irradiance">Energy influx and emittance</a> are expressed in <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Square_meter" title="Square meter">square meter</a> (W/m<sup>2</sup>).</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>greenhouse gas</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> that <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">absorbs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Emission (electromagnetic radiation)">emits</a> <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">radiant energy</a> within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_infrared" title="Thermal infrared">thermal infrared</a> range. Increasing greenhouse gas emissions cause the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4-SYR_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4-SYR-1">[1]</a></sup> The primary greenhouse gases in <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a> are <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>.  Without greenhouse gases, the average temperature of <a href="/wiki/Earth#Surface" title="Earth">Earth's surface</a> would be about −18 °C (0 °F),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> rather than the present average of 15 °C (59 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h2o_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-h2o-5">[5]</a></sup>  The atmospheres of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Mars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">Titan</a> also contain greenhouse gases.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Human_behavior" title="Human behavior">Human activities</a> since the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> (around 1750) have produced a 40% increase in the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">atmospheric concentration of carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>), from 280 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> in 1750 to 406 ppm in early 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> This increase has occurred despite the uptake of more than half of the emissions by various natural "sinks" involved in the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cdiac_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-cdiac-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>  The vast majority of <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a> carbon dioxide emissions (i.e., emissions produced by human activities) come from <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, principally  <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, with additional contributions coming from deforestation, changes in land use, soil erosion and agriculture (including livestock).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Should greenhouse gas emissions continue at their rate in 2017, Earth's surface temperature could exceed historical values as early as 2047, with potentially harmful <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effects on ecosystems, biodiversity and human livelihoods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> At current emission rates temperatures could increase by 2 °C, which the United Nations' <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">IPCC</a> designated as the upper limit to avoid "dangerous" levels, by 2036.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span id="Gases_in_Earth.27s_atmosphere"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gases_in_Earth's_atmosphere">Gases in Earth's atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Gases in Earth's atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">Greenhouse effect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Atmosphere of Earth</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atmospheric_Transmission.png"><img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="857" data-file-width="850" height="444" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Atmospheric_Transmission.png/440px-Atmospheric_Transmission.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Atmospheric_Transmission.png/660px-Atmospheric_Transmission.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Atmospheric_Transmission.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atmospheric_Transmission.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Atmospheric absorption and scattering at different <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic waves</a>. The largest absorption band of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> is not far from the maximum in the <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">thermal emission</a> from ground, and it partly closes the window of transparency of water; hence its major effect.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gases">Greenhouse gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Greenhouse gases are those that absorb and emit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_infrared" title="Thermal infrared">infrared radiation in the wavelength range emitted by Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4-SYR_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4-SYR-1">[1]</a></sup> In order, the most abundant greenhouse gases in Earth's atmosphere are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">Water vapor</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">Nitrous oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">Ozone</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">Chlorofluorocarbons</a> (CFCs)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbons" title="Hydrofluorocarbons">Hydrofluorocarbons</a> (incl. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HCFCs" title="HCFCs">HCFCs</a> and HFCs)</li></ul>
<p>Atmospheric concentrations are determined by the balance between sources (emissions of the gas from human activities and natural systems) and sinks (the removal of the gas from the atmosphere by conversion to a different chemical compound or absorption by bodies of water).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_WG1_AR4_Ch7_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_WG1_AR4_Ch7-13">[13]</a></sup> The proportion of an emission remaining in the atmosphere after a specified time is the "<a href="/wiki/Airborne_fraction" title="Airborne fraction">airborne fraction</a>" (AF). The <i>annual airborne fraction</i> is the ratio of the atmospheric increase in a given year to that year's total emissions. As of 2006 the annual airborne fraction for CO<sub>2</sub> was about 0.45. The annual airborne fraction increased at a rate of 0.25 ± 0.21% per year over the period 1959–2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canadell2007_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canadell2007-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-greenhouse_gases">Non-greenhouse gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Non-greenhouse gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The major atmospheric constituents, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), and <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> (Ar), are not greenhouse gases because <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">molecules containing two atoms of the same element</a> such as <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> have no net change in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_dipole_moment" title="Molecular dipole moment">distribution of their electrical charges</a> when they vibrate, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monatomic" title="Monatomic">monatomic</a> gases such as Ar do not have vibrational modes. Hence they are <a href="/wiki/Selection_rule#Vibrational_spectra" title="Selection rule">almost totally unaffected</a> by <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared radiation</a>. Some heterodiatomic molecules containing atoms of different elements such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO) or <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chloride" title="Hydrogen chloride">hydrogen chloride</a> (HCl) do absorb infrared radiation, although these molecules are short-lived in the atmosphere owing to their reactivity and solubility. Therefore, they do not contribute significantly to the greenhouse effect and often are omitted when discussing greenhouse gases.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_radiative_effects">Indirect radiative effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Indirect radiative effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg"><img alt="world map of carbon monoxide concentrations in the lower atmosphere" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="540" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg/200px-Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg/300px-Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg/400px-Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mopitt_first_year_carbon_monoxide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The false colors in this image represent concentrations of carbon monoxide in the lower atmosphere, ranging from about 390 parts per billion (dark brown pixels), to 220 parts per billion (red pixels), to 50 parts per billion (blue pixels).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Some gases have indirect radiative effects (whether or not they are greenhouse gases themselves). This happens in two main ways. One way is that when they break down in the atmosphere they produce another greenhouse gas. For example, methane and carbon monoxide (CO) are oxidized to give carbon dioxide (and methane oxidation also produces water vapor). Oxidation of CO to CO<sub>2</sub> directly produces an unambiguous increase in radiative forcing although the reason is subtle. The peak of the thermal IR emission from Earth's surface is very close to a strong vibrational absorption band of CO<sub>2</sub> (15 microns, or 667 cm<sup>−1</sup>). On the other hand, the single CO vibrational band only absorbs IR at much shorter wavelengths (4.7 microns, or 2145 cm<sup>−1</sup>), where the emission of radiant energy from Earth's surface is at least a factor of ten lower.  Oxidation of methane to CO<sub>2</sub>, which requires reactions with the OH radical, produces an instantaneous reduction in radiative absorption and emission since CO<sub>2</sub> is a weaker greenhouse gas than methane. However, the oxidations of CO and <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> are entwined since both consume OH radicals. In any case, the calculation of the total radiative effect includes both direct and indirect forcing.
</p><p>A second type of indirect effect happens when chemical reactions in the atmosphere involving these gases change the concentrations of greenhouse gases. For example, the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Non-methane_volatile_organic_compound" title="Non-methane volatile organic compound">non-methane volatile organic compounds</a> (NMVOCs) in the atmosphere can produce ozone. The size of the indirect effect can depend strongly on where and when the gas is emitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forsteretal_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-forsteretal-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Methane has indirect effects in addition to forming CO<sub>2</sub>. The main chemical that reacts with methane in the atmosphere is the <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">hydroxyl radical</a> (OH), thus more methane means that the concentration of OH goes down. Effectively, methane increases its own atmospheric lifetime and therefore its overall radiative effect. The oxidation of methane can produce both ozone and water; and is a major source of water vapor in the normally dry <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>. CO and NMVOCs produce CO<sub>2</sub> when they are oxidized. They remove OH from the atmosphere, and this leads to higher concentrations of methane. The surprising effect of this is that the global warming potential of CO is three times that of CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maccartyetal_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-maccartyetal-17">[17]</a></sup> The same process that converts NMVOCs to carbon dioxide can also lead to the formation of tropospheric ozone. <a href="/wiki/Halocarbon" title="Halocarbon">Halocarbons</a> have an indirect effect because they destroy stratospheric ozone. Finally, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> can lead to ozone production and <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> increases as well as producing stratospheric water vapor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forsteretal_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-forsteretal-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Contribution_of_clouds_to_Earth.27s_greenhouse_effect"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Contribution_of_clouds_to_Earth's_greenhouse_effect">Contribution of clouds to Earth's greenhouse effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Contribution of clouds to Earth's greenhouse effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The major non-gas contributor to Earth's greenhouse effect, <a href="/wiki/Cloud_forcing" title="Cloud forcing">clouds</a>, also absorb and emit infrared radiation and thus have an effect on greenhouse gas radiative properties. Clouds are water droplets or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_crystal" title="Ice crystal">ice crystals</a> suspended in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kiehl197_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-kiehl197-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-realclimate.org_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-realclimate.org-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impacts_on_the_overall_greenhouse_effect">Impacts on the overall greenhouse effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Impacts on the overall greenhouse effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect,_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_(NASA).png"><img alt="refer to caption and adjacent text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2333" data-file-width="3266" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png/220px-Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png/330px-Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png/440px-Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect%2C_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_%28NASA%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Attribution_of_individual_atmospheric_component_contributions_to_the_terrestrial_greenhouse_effect,_separated_into_feedback_and_forcing_categories_(NASA).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schmidt <i>et al.</i> (2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> analysed how individual components of the atmosphere contribute to the total greenhouse effect. They estimated that water vapor accounts for about 50% of Earth's greenhouse effect, with clouds contributing 25%, carbon dioxide 20%, and the minor greenhouse gases and <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosols</a> accounting for the remaining 5%. In the study, the reference model atmosphere is for 1980 conditions. Image credit: <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The contribution of each gas to the greenhouse effect is determined by the characteristics of that gas, its abundance, and any indirect effects it may cause. For example, the direct radiative effect of a mass of methane is about 84 times stronger than the same mass of carbon dioxide over a 20-year time frame<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TableOfWarmingPotentials5_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-TableOfWarmingPotentials5-22">[22]</a></sup> but it is present in much smaller concentrations so that its total direct radiative effect is smaller, in part due to its shorter atmospheric lifetime. On the other hand, in addition to its direct radiative impact, methane has a large, indirect radiative effect because it contributes to ozone formation. Shindell <i>et al.</i> (2005)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> argue that the contribution to climate change from methane is at least double previous estimates as a result of this effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>When ranked by their direct contribution to the greenhouse effect, the most important are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kiehl197_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-kiehl197-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (April 2016)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
<tbody><tr>

</th>

</th>
<th>Contribution <br/> (%)
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b><a href="/wiki/Water_vapor#In_Earth's_atmosphere" title="Water vapor">Water vapor</a> and clouds</b></td>

</p>
</th></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In addition to the main greenhouse gases listed above, other greenhouse gases include <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">sulfur hexafluoride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbon" title="Hydrofluorocarbon">hydrofluorocarbons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfluorocarbon" title="Perfluorocarbon">perfluorocarbons</a> (see <a href="/wiki/IPCC_list_of_greenhouse_gases" title="IPCC list of greenhouse gases">IPCC list of greenhouse gases</a>). Some greenhouse gases are not often listed. For example, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_trifluoride" title="Nitrogen trifluoride">nitrogen trifluoride</a> has a high <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> (GWP) but is only present in very small quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NF3_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NF3-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proportion_of_direct_effects_at_a_given_moment">Proportion of direct effects at a given moment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Proportion of direct effects at a given moment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is not possible to state that a certain gas causes an exact percentage of the greenhouse effect. This is because some of the gases absorb and emit radiation at the same frequencies as others, so that the total greenhouse effect is not simply the sum of the influence of each gas. The higher ends of the ranges quoted are for each gas alone; the lower ends account for overlaps with the other gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kiehl197_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-kiehl197-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-realclimate.org_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-realclimate.org-19">[19]</a></sup> In addition, some gases, such as methane, are known to have large indirect effects that are still being quantified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_lifetime">Atmospheric lifetime</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Atmospheric lifetime">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aside from <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, which has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Residence_time_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Residence time (fluid dynamics)">residence time</a> of about nine days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> major greenhouse gases are well mixed and take many years to leave the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-betts_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-betts-30">[30]</a></sup> Although it is not easy to know with precision how long it takes greenhouse gases to leave the atmosphere, there are estimates for the principal greenhouse gases.
Jacob (1999)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JacobDJ1999_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-JacobDJ1999-31">[31]</a></sup> defines the lifetime <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>τ<!-- τ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \tau }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\tau " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/38a7dcde9730ef0853809fefc18d88771f95206c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.202ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> of an atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Chemical_species" title="Chemical species">species</a> X in a one-<a href="/wiki/Climate_model#Box_models" title="Climate model">box model</a> as the average time that a molecule of X remains in the box. Mathematically <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mi>τ<!-- τ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \tau }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\tau " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/38a7dcde9730ef0853809fefc18d88771f95206c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.202ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> can
be defined as the ratio of the mass <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

(<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F_{out}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</math></span><img alt="F_{{out}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/010777ed1b8fe2ac62e41ec617400d95065c5bec" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.058ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>),
chemical loss of X
(<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle L}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

and <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(chemistry)" title="Deposition (chemistry)">deposition</a> of X
(<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle D}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

(all in kg/s):
<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau ={\frac {m}{F_{out}+L+D}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\tau ={\frac  {m}{F_{{out}}+L+D}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/181e5432246a85f54af3b8a2defb06fa88fe8d38" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:18.383ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JacobDJ1999_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-JacobDJ1999-31">[31]</a></sup>
If output of this gas into the box ceased, then after time <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>τ<!-- τ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \tau }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\tau " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/38a7dcde9730ef0853809fefc18d88771f95206c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.202ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>, its concentration would decrease by about 63%.
</p><p>The atmospheric lifetime of a species therefore measures the time required to restore equilibrium following a sudden increase or decrease in its concentration in the atmosphere. Individual atoms or molecules may be lost or deposited to sinks such as the soil, the oceans and other waters, or vegetation and other biological systems, reducing the excess to background concentrations. The average time taken to achieve this is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a> has a variable atmospheric lifetime, and cannot be specified precisely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The atmospheric lifetime of CO<sub>2</sub> is estimated of the order of 30–95 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JacobsonMZ2005_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-JacobsonMZ2005-33">[33]</a></sup>
This figure accounts for CO<sub>2</sub> molecules being removed from the atmosphere by mixing into the ocean, photosynthesis, and other processes. However, this excludes the balancing fluxes of CO<sub>2</sub> into the atmosphere from the geological reservoirs, which have slower characteristic rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArcherD2009_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArcherD2009-34">[34]</a></sup> Although more than half of the CO<sub>2</sub> emitted is removed from the atmosphere within a century, some fraction (about 20%) of emitted CO<sub>2</sub> remains in the atmosphere for many thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carbon_lifetime1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-carbon_lifetime1-36">[36]</a></sup>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carbon_lifetime2_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-carbon_lifetime2-37">[37]</a></sup> Similar issues apply to other greenhouse gases, many of which have longer mean lifetimes than CO<sub>2</sub>, e.g. <a href="#N2O">N<sub>2</sub>O</a> has a mean atmospheric lifetime of 121 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TableOfWarmingPotentials5_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-TableOfWarmingPotentials5-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiative_forcing">Radiative forcing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Radiative forcing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Earth absorbs some of the radiant energy received from the sun, reflects some of it as light and reflects or radiates the rest back to space as <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">heat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">[38]</a></sup> Earth's surface temperature depends on this balance between incoming and outgoing energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">[38]</a></sup> If this <a href="/wiki/First_law_of_thermodynamics" title="First law of thermodynamics">energy balance</a> is shifted, Earth's surface becomes warmer or cooler, leading to a variety of changes in global climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of natural and man-made mechanisms can affect the global energy balance and force changes in Earth's climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">[38]</a></sup> Greenhouse gases are one such mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">[38]</a></sup> Greenhouse gases absorb and emit some of the outgoing energy radiated from Earth's surface, causing that heat to be retained in the lower atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-5"><a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">[38]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Atmospheric_lifetime" title="Greenhouse gas">explained above</a>, some greenhouse gases remain in the atmosphere for decades or even centuries, and therefore can affect Earth's energy balance over a long period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-6"><a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">Radiative forcing</a> quantifies the effect of factors that influence Earth's energy balance, including changes in the concentrations of greenhouse gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-7"><a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">[38]</a></sup> Positive radiative forcing leads to warming by increasing the net incoming energy, whereas negative radiative forcing leads to cooling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_radiative_forcing_38-8"><a href="#cite_note-epa_radiative_forcing-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_warming_potential">Global warming potential</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Global warming potential">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> (GWP) depends on both the efficiency of the molecule as a greenhouse gas and its atmospheric lifetime. GWP is measured relative to the same <b>mass</b> of CO<sub>2</sub> and evaluated for a specific timescale. Thus, if a gas has a high (positive) <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">radiative forcing</a> but also a short lifetime, it will have a large GWP on a 20-year scale but a small one on a 100-year scale. Conversely, if a molecule has a longer atmospheric lifetime than CO<sub>2</sub> its GWP will increase when the timescale is considered. Carbon dioxide is defined to have a GWP of 1 over all time periods.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> has an atmospheric lifetime of 12 ± 3 years. The <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">2007 IPCC report</a> lists the GWP as 72 over a time scale of 20 years, 25 over 100 years and 7.6 over 500 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TableOfWarmingPotentials_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-TableOfWarmingPotentials-39">[39]</a></sup> A 2014 analysis, however, states that although methane's initial impact is about 100 times greater than that of CO<sub>2</sub>, because of the shorter atmospheric lifetime, after six or seven decades, the impact of the two gases is about equal, and from then on methane's relative role continues to decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The decrease in GWP at longer times is because <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane#Removal_processes" title="Atmospheric methane">methane</a> is degraded to water and CO<sub>2</sub> through chemical reactions in the atmosphere.
</p><p>Examples of the atmospheric lifetime and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">GWP</a> relative to CO<sub>2</sub> for several greenhouse gases are given in the following table:

<p>The use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CFC-12" title="CFC-12">CFC-12</a> (except some essential uses) has been phased out due to its <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">ozone depleting</a> properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The phasing-out of less active <a href="/wiki/Haloalkane" title="Haloalkane">HCFC-compounds</a> will be completed in 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_and_anthropogenic_sources">Natural and anthropogenic sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Natural and anthropogenic sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png"><img alt="refer to caption and article text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="642" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png/220px-Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png/330px-Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png/440px-Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_History_and_Flux_Rev.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Top: Increasing atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> levels as measured in the atmosphere and reflected in <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice cores</a>. Bottom: The amount of net carbon increase in the atmosphere, compared to carbon emissions from burning <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_(US_DOE).png"><img alt="refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2192" data-file-width="2600" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png/220px-Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png/330px-Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png/440px-Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_%28US_DOE%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diagram_showing_a_simplified_representation_of_the_Earth%27s_annual_carbon_cycle_(US_DOE).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This diagram shows a simplified representation of the contemporary global <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>. Changes are measured in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigaton" title="Gigaton">gigatons</a> of carbon per year (GtC/y). Canadell <i>et al.</i> (2007) estimated the growth rate of global average atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> for 2000–2006 as 1.93 parts-per-million per year (4.1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petagram" title="Petagram">petagrams</a> of carbon per year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Aside from purely human-produced synthetic halocarbons, most greenhouse gases have both natural and human-caused sources. During the pre-industrial <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>, concentrations of existing gases were roughly constant, because the large natural sources and sinks roughly balanced. In the industrial era, human activities have added greenhouse gases to the atmosphere, mainly through the burning of fossil fuels and clearing of forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2007 <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">Fourth Assessment Report</a> compiled by the IPCC (AR4) noted that "changes in atmospheric concentrations of greenhouse gases and aerosols, land cover and solar radiation alter the energy balance of the climate system", and concluded that "increases in anthropogenic greenhouse gas concentrations is very likely to have caused most of the increases in global average temperatures since the mid-20th century".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> In AR4, "most of" is defined as more than 50%.
</p><p><i>Abbreviations used in the two tables below: ppm = <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">parts-per-million</a>; ppb = parts-per-billion; ppt = parts-per-trillion; W/m<sup>2</sup> = <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> per <a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">square metre</a></i>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vostok_Petit_data.svg"><img alt="refer to caption and article text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/220px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/330px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/440px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vostok_Petit_data.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>400,000 years of ice core data</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">Ice cores</a> provide evidence for greenhouse gas concentration variations over the past 800,000 years (see the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Ice_cores" title="Greenhouse gas">following section</a>). Both CO<sub>2</sub> and <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> vary between glacial and interglacial phases, and concentrations of these gases correlate strongly with temperature. Direct data does not exist for periods earlier than those represented in the ice core record, a record that indicates CO<sub>2</sub> <a href="/wiki/Mole_fraction" title="Mole fraction">mole fractions</a> stayed within a range of 180 ppm to 280 ppm throughout the last 800,000 years, until the increase of the last 250 years. However, various proxies and modeling suggests larger variations in past epochs; 500 million years ago CO<sub>2</sub> levels were likely 10 times higher than now.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Indeed, higher CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations are thought to have prevailed throughout most of the <a href="/wiki/Phanerozoic" title="Phanerozoic">Phanerozoic</a> eon, with concentrations four to six times current concentrations during the Mesozoic era, and ten to fifteen times current concentrations during the early Palaeozoic era until the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> period, about 400 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">Ma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berner1994_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berner1994-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Royeretal2001_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Royeretal2001-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berner&amp;Kothavala2001_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berner&amp;Kothavala2001-65">[65]</a></sup> The spread of land plants is thought to have reduced CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations during the late Devonian, and plant activities as both sources and sinks of CO<sub>2</sub> have since been important in providing stabilising feedbacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beerling2005_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beerling2005-66">[66]</a></sup>
Earlier still, a 200-million year period of intermittent, widespread glaciation extending close to the equator (<a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">Snowball Earth</a>) appears to have been ended suddenly, about 550 Ma, by a colossal volcanic outgassing that raised the CO<sub>2</sub> concentration of the atmosphere abruptly to 12%, about 350 times modern levels, causing extreme greenhouse conditions and carbonate deposition as <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> at the rate of about 1 mm per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffmannetal1998_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffmannetal1998-67">[67]</a></sup> This episode marked the close of the Precambrian eon, and was succeeded by the generally warmer conditions of the Phanerozoic, during which multicellular animal and plant life evolved. No volcanic carbon dioxide emission of comparable scale has occurred since. In the modern era, emissions to the atmosphere from volcanoes are approximately 0.645 billion tons of CO<sub>2</sub> per year, whereas humans contribute 29 billion tons of CO<sub>2</sub> each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffmannetal1998_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffmannetal1998-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gerlach1991_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-gerlach1991-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ice_cores">Ice cores</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ice cores">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere#Past_variation" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">Measurements from Antarctic ice cores</a>
show that before industrial emissions started atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> mole fractions were about 280 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> (ppm), and stayed between 260 and 280 during the preceding ten thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Carbon dioxide mole fractions in the atmosphere have gone up by approximately 35 percent since the 1900s, rising from 280 parts per million by volume to 387 parts per million in 2009. One study using evidence from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">stomata</a> of fossilized leaves suggests greater variability, with carbon dioxide mole fractions above 300 ppm during the period seven to ten thousand years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> though others have argued that these findings more likely reflect calibration or contamination problems rather than actual CO<sub>2</sub> variability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Because of the way air is trapped in ice (pores in the ice close off slowly to form bubbles deep within the firn) and the time period represented in each ice sample analyzed, these figures represent averages of atmospheric concentrations of up to a few centuries rather than annual or decadal levels.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_since_the_Industrial_Revolution">Changes since the Industrial Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Changes since the Industrial Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CO2_increase_rate.png"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="690" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/CO2_increase_rate.png/220px-CO2_increase_rate.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/CO2_increase_rate.png/330px-CO2_increase_rate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/CO2_increase_rate.png/440px-CO2_increase_rate.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CO2_increase_rate.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Recent year-to-year increase of atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="1012" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png/220px-Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png/330px-Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png/440px-Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Major_greenhouse_gas_trends.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major greenhouse gas trends.</div></div></div>
<p>Since the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the concentrations of most of the greenhouse gases have increased. For example, the mole fraction of carbon dioxide has increased from 280 ppm to 400 ppm, or 120 ppm over modern pre-industrial levels. The first 30 ppm increase took place in about 200 years, from the start of the Industrial Revolution to 1958; however the next 90 ppm increase took place within 56 years, from 1958 to 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kibert2016_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kibert2016-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent data also shows that the concentration is increasing at a higher rate. In the 1960s, the average annual increase was only 37% of what it was in 2000 through 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, the stock of carbon in the atmosphere increases by more than 3 million tonnes per annum (0.04%) compared with the existing stock.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="in what year was this measurement made? (April 2011)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This increase is the result of human activities by burning fossil fuels, deforestation and forest degradation in tropical and boreal regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tarziu2011_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tarziu2011-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>The other greenhouse gases produced from human activity show similar increases in both amount and rate of increase. Many observations are available online in a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_Chemistry_Observational_Databases" title="Atmospheric Chemistry Observational Databases">Atmospheric Chemistry Observational Databases</a>.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropogenic_greenhouse_gases">Anthropogenic greenhouse gases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Anthropogenic greenhouse gases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_ecosystems" title="Climate change and ecosystems">climate change and ecosystems</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="801" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/51/NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png/250px-NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/51/NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png/375px-NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/51/NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png/500px-NOAA_Annual_Greenhouse_Gas_Index_2012.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">This graph shows changes in the annual greenhouse gas index (AGGI) between 1979 and 2011.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annual_greenhouse_gas_index_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-annual_greenhouse_gas_index-79">[79]</a></sup> The AGGI measures the levels of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere based on their ability to cause changes in Earth's climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annual_greenhouse_gas_index_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-annual_greenhouse_gas_index-79">[79]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector,_1990-2005,_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_(EPA,_2010).png"><img alt="" data-file-height="610" data-file-width="670" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png/250px-Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png/375px-Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png/500px-Global_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_1990-2005%2C_in_carbon_dioxide_equivalents_%28EPA%2C_2010%29.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">This bar graph shows global greenhouse gas emissions by sector from 1990 to 2005, measured in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide equivalents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/250px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/375px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg/500px-Global_Carbon_Emissions.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Modern global CO2 emissions from the burning of fossil fuels.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Since about 1750 human activity has increased the concentration of carbon dioxide and other greenhouse gases. Measured atmospheric concentrations of carbon dioxide are currently 100 ppm higher than pre-industrial levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Natural sources of carbon dioxide are more than 20 times greater than sources due to human activity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> but over periods longer than a few years natural sources are closely balanced by natural sinks, mainly photosynthesis of carbon compounds by plants and marine plankton. As a result of this balance, the atmospheric mole fraction of carbon dioxide remained between 260 and 280 parts per million for the 10,000 years between the end of the last glacial maximum and the start of the industrial era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4_WG1_Chapter_7_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4_WG1_Chapter_7-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is likely that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropogenic_greenhouse_gases" title="Anthropogenic greenhouse gases">anthropogenic</a> (i.e., human-induced) warming, such as that due to elevated greenhouse gas levels, has had a discernible influence on many physical and biological systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Future warming is projected to have a range of <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">impacts</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-projected_impacts_of_climate_change-85">[85]</a></sup> increased frequencies and severities of some <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">extreme weather</a> events,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-projected_impacts_of_climate_change-85">[85]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_of_biodiversity" title="Loss of biodiversity">loss of biodiversity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_by_sector_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_by_sector-86">[86]</a></sup> and regional changes in <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_agriculture" title="Climate change and agriculture">agricultural productivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_by_sector_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-projected_impacts_of_climate_change_by_sector-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main sources of greenhouse gases due to human activity are:
</p>
<ul><li>burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> leading to higher carbon dioxide concentrations in the air. Land use change (mainly deforestation in the tropics) account for up to one third of total anthropogenic CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_AR4_WG1_Chapter_7_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_AR4_WG1_Chapter_7-83">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li>livestock <a href="/wiki/Enteric_fermentation" title="Enteric fermentation">enteric fermentation</a> and manure management,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livestock_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-livestock-87">[87]</a></sup> paddy <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> farming, land use and wetland changes, man-made lakes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_5th_Assessment,_Chp._6_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_5th_Assessment,_Chp._6-88">[88]</a></sup> pipeline losses, and covered vented landfill emissions leading to higher methane atmospheric concentrations. Many of the newer style fully vented septic systems that enhance and target the fermentation process also are sources of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">atmospheric methane</a>.</li>
<li>use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbons" title="Chlorofluorocarbons">chlorofluorocarbons</a> (CFCs) in <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration" title="Refrigeration">refrigeration</a> systems, and use of CFCs and <a href="/wiki/Halomethane#Fire_extinguishing" title="Halomethane">halons</a> in <a href="/wiki/Fire_extinguisher" title="Fire extinguisher">fire suppression</a> systems and manufacturing processes.</li>
<li>agricultural activities, including the use of fertilizers, that lead to higher nitrous oxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) concentrations.</li></ul>
<p>The seven sources of CO<sub>2</sub> from fossil fuel combustion are (with percentage contributions for 2000–2004):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raupach_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raupach-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">

<td>36%
