<td> 4 %
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) and three groups of <a href="/wiki/Fluorinated_gases" title="Fluorinated gases">fluorinated gases</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">sulfur hexafluoride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">SF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbons" title="Hydrofluorocarbons">hydrofluorocarbons</a> (HFCs), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfluorocarbons" title="Perfluorocarbons">perfluorocarbons</a> (PFCs)) are the major anthropogenic greenhouse gases,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_kyoto_protocol_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_kyoto_protocol-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>147</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Environmental_issues:_essential_primary_sources._92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Environmental_issues:_essential_primary_sources.-92">[92]</a></sup> and are regulated under the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> international <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaty</a>, which came into force in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfccc_kyoto_protocol_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-unfccc_kyoto_protocol-93">[93]</a></sup> Emissions limitations specified in the Kyoto Protocol expired in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unfccc_kyoto_protocol_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-unfccc_kyoto_protocol-93">[93]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Nations_Climate_Change_Conference" title="2010 United Nations Climate Change Conference">Cancún agreement</a>, agreed on in 2010, includes voluntary pledges made by 76 countries to control emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancun_agreement_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-cancun_agreement-94">[94]</a></sup> At the time of the agreement, these 76 countries were collectively responsible for 85% of annual global emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cancun_agreement_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-cancun_agreement-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CFCs" title="CFCs">CFCs</a> are greenhouse gases, they are regulated by the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a>, which was motivated by CFCs' contribution to <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">ozone depletion</a> rather than by their contribution to global warming. Note that ozone depletion has only a minor role in greenhouse warming, though the two processes often are confused in the media. On 15 October 2016, negotiators from over 170 nations meeting at the summit of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> reached a legally binding accord to phase out <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbons" title="Hydrofluorocarbons">hydrofluorocarbons</a> (HFCs) in an amendment to the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sectors">Sectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sectors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b> with: Information on emissions from other sectors. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(July 2013)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="603" data-file-width="650" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png/220px-Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png/330px-Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png/440px-Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_Gas_by_Sector.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This figure shows the relative fraction of anthropogenic greenhouse gases coming from each of eight categories of sources, as estimated by the Emission Database for Global Atmospheric Research version 3.2, fast track 2000 project [1]. These values are intended to provide a snapshot of global annual greenhouse gas emissions in the year 2000. The top panel shows the sum over all anthropogenic greenhouse gases, weighted by their global warming potential over the next 100 years. This consists of 72% carbon dioxide, 18% methane, 8% nitrous oxide and 1% other gases. Lower panels show the comparable information for each of these three primary greenhouse gases, with the same coloring of sectors as used in the top chart. Segments with less than 1% fraction are not labeled.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNEP" title="UNEP">UNEP</a> global <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourism</a> is closely linked to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>. Tourism is a significant contributor to the increasing concentrations of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere. Tourism accounts for about 50% of traffic movements. Rapidly expanding air traffic contributes about 2.5% of the production of CO<sub>2</sub>. The number of international travelers is expected to increase from 594 million in 1996 to 1.6 billion by 2020, adding greatly to the problem unless steps are taken to reduce emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trucking_and_haulage">Trucking and haulage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Trucking and haulage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Road_transport#Trucking_and_haulage" title="Road transport">trucking and haulage industry</a> plays a part in production of CO<sub>2</sub>, contributing around 20% of the UK's total carbon emissions a year, with only the energy industry having a larger impact at around 39%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
Average carbon emissions within the haulage industry are falling—in the thirty-year period from 1977 to 2007, the carbon emissions associated with a 200-mile journey fell by 21 percent; NOx emissions are also down 87 percent, whereas journey times have fallen by around a third.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Due to their size, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_goods_vehicle" title="Heavy goods vehicle">HGVs</a> often receive criticism regarding their CO2 emissions; however, rapid development in engine technology and fuel management is having a largely positive effect.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plastic">Plastic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Plastic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Plastic is produced mainly from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">Fossil fuels</a>. Plastic manufacturing is estimated to use 8 percent of yearly global oil production. The EPA estimates as many as five ounces of carbon dioxide are emitted for each ounce of polyethylene (PET) produced—the type of plastic most commonly used for beverage bottles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plastic_Pollution_and_Climate_Change_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plastic_Pollution_and_Climate_Change-101">[101]</a></sup> the transportation produce greenhouse gases also.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_Is_the_Carbon_Footprint_of_a_Plastic_Bottle?_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-What_Is_the_Carbon_Footprint_of_a_Plastic_Bottle?-102">[102]</a></sup> Plastic waste emits carbon dioxide when it degrades. In 2018 research claimed that some of the most common plastics in the environment release the greenhouse gasses <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethylene" title="Ethylene">Ethylene</a> when exposed to sunlight in an amount that can affect the earth climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Production_of_methane_and_ethylene_from_plastic_in_the_environment_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Production_of_methane_and_ethylene_from_plastic_in_the_environment-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Study_Finds_New_Reason_to_Ban_Plastic:_It_Emits_Methane_in_the_Sun_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Study_Finds_New_Reason_to_Ban_Plastic:_It_Emits_Methane_in_the_Sun-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the other side, if it is placed in a landfill, it becomes a carbon sink<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> although biodegradable plastics have caused <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Due to the lightness of plastic versus glass or metal, plastic may reduce energy consumption. For example, packaging beverages in PET plastic rather than glass or metal is estimated to save 52% in transportation energy, if the glass or metal packag is single use, of course .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Applications_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Applications-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_water_vapor">Role of water vapor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Role of water vapor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="775" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png/350px-BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png/525px-BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png/700px-BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BAMS_climate_assess_boulder_water_vapor_2002.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Increasing water vapor in the stratosphere at Boulder, Colorado.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">Water vapor</a> accounts for the largest percentage of the greenhouse effect, between 36% and 66% for clear sky conditions and between 66% and 85% when including clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-realclimate.org_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-realclimate.org-19">[19]</a></sup> Water vapor concentrations fluctuate regionally, but human activity does not directly affect water vapor concentrations except at local scales, such as near irrigated fields. Indirectly, human activity that increases global temperatures will increase water vapor concentrations, a process known as water vapor feedback.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The atmospheric concentration of vapor is highly variable and depends largely on temperature, from less than 0.01% in extremely cold regions up to 3% by mass in saturated air at about 32 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> (See <a href="/wiki/Relative_humidity#other_important_facts" title="Relative humidity">Relative humidity#other important facts</a>.)
</p><p>The average residence time of a water molecule in the atmosphere is only about nine days, compared to years or centuries for other greenhouse gases such as <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> and CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Thus, water vapor responds to and amplifies effects of the other greenhouse gases. The <a href="/wiki/Clausius%E2%80%93Clapeyron_relation" title="Clausius–Clapeyron relation">Clausius–Clapeyron relation</a> establishes that more water vapor will be present per unit volume at elevated temperatures. This and other basic principles indicate that warming associated with increased concentrations of the other greenhouse gases also will increase the concentration of water vapor (assuming that the <a href="/wiki/Relative_humidity" title="Relative humidity">relative humidity</a> remains approximately constant; modeling and observational studies find that this is indeed so). Because water vapor is a greenhouse gas, this results in further warming and so is a "<a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a>" that amplifies the original warming. Eventually other earth processes offset these positive feedbacks, stabilizing the global temperature at a new equilibrium and preventing the loss of Earth's water through a Venus-like <a href="/wiki/Runaway_greenhouse_effect" title="Runaway greenhouse effect">runaway greenhouse effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_greenhouse_gas_emissions">Direct greenhouse gas emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Direct greenhouse gas emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Between the period 1970 to 2004, greenhouse gas emissions (measured in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO<sub>2</sub>-equivalent</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> increased at an average rate of 1.6% per year, with CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from the use of fossil fuels growing at a rate of 1.9% per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Total anthropogenic emissions at the end of 2009 were estimated at 49.5 <a href="/wiki/Giga-" title="Giga-">gigatonnes</a> CO<sub>2</sub>-equivalent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bridging_the_Emissions_Gap_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bridging_the_Emissions_Gap-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>15</span></sup> These emissions include CO<sub>2</sub> from fossil fuel use and from land use, as well as emissions of methane, nitrous oxide and other greenhouse gases covered by the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a>.
</p><p>At present, the primary source of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions is the burning of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> for electricity and heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_and_national_attribution_of_emissions">Regional and national attribution of emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Regional and national attribution of emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol_and_government_action" title="Kyoto Protocol and government action">Kyoto Protocol and government action</a></div>
<p>According to the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA), GHG emissions in the United States can be traced from different sectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_is_GHG?_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-What_is_GHG?-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are several different ways of measuring greenhouse gas emissions, for example, see World Bank (2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_emissions_data_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-world_bank_emissions_data-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>362</span></sup> for tables of national emissions data. Some variables that have been reported<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sapiens.revues.org_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-sapiens.revues.org-119">[119]</a></sup> include:
</p>
<ul><li>Definition of measurement boundaries: Emissions can be attributed geographically, to the area where they were emitted (the territory principle) or by the activity principle to the territory produced the emissions. These two principles result in different totals when measuring, for example, electricity importation from one country to another, or emissions at an international airport.</li>
<li>Time horizon of different gases: Contribution of a given greenhouse gas is reported as a CO<sub>2</sub> equivalent. The calculation to determine this takes into account how long that gas remains in the atmosphere. This is not always known accurately and calculations must be regularly updated to reflect new information.</li>
<li>What sectors are included in the calculation (e.g., energy industries, industrial processes, agriculture etc.): There is often a conflict between transparency and availability of data.</li>
<li>The measurement protocol itself: This may be via direct measurement or estimation. The four main methods are the emission factor-based method, mass balance method, predictive emissions monitoring systems, and continuous emissions monitoring systems. These methods differ in accuracy, cost, and usability.</li></ul>
<p>These different measures are sometimes used by different countries to assert various policy/ethical positions on climate change (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, p. 94).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-120">[120]</a></sup>
The use of different measures leads to a lack of comparability, which is problematic when monitoring progress towards targets. There are arguments for the adoption of a common measurement tool, or at least the development of communication between different tools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sapiens.revues.org_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-sapiens.revues.org-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Emissions may be measured over long time periods. This measurement type is called historical or cumulative emissions. Cumulative emissions give some indication of who is responsible for the build-up in the atmospheric concentration of greenhouse gases (IEA, 2007, p. 199).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iea_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-iea-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The national accounts balance would be positively related to carbon emissions. The national accounts balance shows the difference between exports and imports. For many richer nations, such as the United States, the accounts balance is negative because more goods are imported than they are exported. This is mostly due to the fact that it is cheaper to produce goods outside of developed countries, leading the economies of developed countries to become increasingly dependent on services and not goods. We believed that a positive accounts balance would means that more production was occurring in a country, so more factories working would increase carbon emission levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holtz-eakin_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-holtz-eakin-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Emissions may also be measured across shorter time periods. Emissions changes may, for example, be measured against a base year of 1990. 1990 was used in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC) as the base year for emissions, and is also used in the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> (some gases are also measured from the year 1995).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_kyoto_protocol_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_kyoto_protocol-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>146, 149</span></sup> A country's emissions may also be reported as a proportion of global emissions for a particular year.
</p><p>Another measurement is of per capita emissions. This divides a country's total annual emissions by its mid-year population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_bank_emissions_data_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-world_bank_emissions_data-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>370</span></sup> Per capita emissions may be based on historical or annual emissions (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, pp. 106–07).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>While cities are sometimes considered to be disproportionate contributors to emissions, per-capita emissions tend to be lower for cities than the averages in their countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_land-use_change">From land-use change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: From land-use change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture,_forestry_and_other_land_use,_1970-2010.png"><img alt="Refer to caption." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="671" data-file-width="1200" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png/220px-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png/330px-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png/440px-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_forestry_and_other_land_use%2C_1970-2010.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture,_forestry_and_other_land_use,_1970-2010.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture, forestry and other land use, 1970–2010.</div></div></div>
<p>Land-use change, e.g., the clearing of forests for agricultural use, can affect the concentration of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere by altering how much carbon flows out of the atmosphere into <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sinks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Accounting for land-use change can be understood as an attempt to measure "net" emissions, i.e., gross emissions from all sources minus the removal of emissions from the atmosphere by carbon sinks (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, pp. 92–93).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_120-2"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are substantial uncertainties in the measurement of net carbon emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Additionally, there is controversy over how carbon sinks should be allocated between different regions and over time (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, p. 93).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_120-3"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-120">[120]</a></sup> For instance, concentrating on more recent changes in carbon sinks is likely to favour those regions that have deforested earlier, e.g., Europe.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gas_intensity">Greenhouse gas intensity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Greenhouse gas intensity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_intensity" title="List of countries by carbon intensity">List of countries by carbon intensity</a></div>
<table>

<td><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GHG_intensity_2000.svg"><img alt="Refer to caption." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/GHG_intensity_2000.svg/170px-GHG_intensity_2000.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/GHG_intensity_2000.svg/255px-GHG_intensity_2000.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/GHG_intensity_2000.svg/340px-GHG_intensity_2000.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GHG_intensity_2000.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greenhouse gas intensity in the year 2000, including land-use change.</div></div></div>

<td><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_(using_PPP)_for_different_regions,_1982-2011.png"><img alt="Refer to caption." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="671" data-file-width="1200" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_PPP%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011.png/170px-Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_PPP%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_PPP%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011.png/255px-Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_PPP%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_PPP%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011.png/340px-Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_PPP%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_(using_PPP)_for_different_regions,_1982-2011.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carbon intensity of GDP (using PPP) for different regions, 1982–2011.</div></div></div>
</td>
<td><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_(using_MER)_for_different_regions,_1982-2011_(corrected).png"><img alt="Refer to caption." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="671" data-file-width="1200" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png/170px-Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png/255px-Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png/340px-Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_%28using_MER%29_for_different_regions%2C_1982-2011_%28corrected%29.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_intensity_of_GDP_(using_MER)_for_different_regions,_1982-2011_(corrected).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carbon intensity of GDP (using MER) for different regions, 1982–2011.</div></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Greenhouse gas intensity is a ratio between greenhouse gas emissions and another metric, e.g., gross domestic product (GDP) or energy use. The terms "carbon intensity" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emissions_intensity" title="Emissions intensity">emissions intensity</a>" are also sometimes used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Emission intensities may be calculated using <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product#Cross-border_comparison" title="Gross domestic product">market exchange rates</a> (MER) or <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> (PPP) (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, p. 96).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_120-4"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-120">[120]</a></sup> Calculations based on MER show large differences in intensities between developed and developing countries, whereas calculations based on PPP show smaller differences.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumulative_and_historical_emissions">Cumulative and historical emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Cumulative and historical emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

2x" width="180"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Regional trends in annual per capita CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from fuel combustion between 1971 and 2009.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Cumulative anthropogenic (i.e., human-emitted) emissions of CO<sub>2</sub> from fossil fuel use are a major cause of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> and give some indication of which countries have contributed most to human-induced climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>15</span></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">

<td style="text-align:left;">OECD Europe</td>

</td></tr>

<p>The table above to the left is based on Banuri <i>et al.</i> (1996, p. 94).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_120-5"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-120">[120]</a></sup> Overall, developed countries accounted for 83.8% of industrial CO<sub>2</sub> emissions over this time period, and 67.8% of total CO<sub>2</sub> emissions. Developing countries accounted for industrial CO<sub>2</sub> emissions of 16.2% over this time period, and 32.2% of total CO<sub>2</sub> emissions. The estimate of total CO<sub>2</sub> emissions includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" title="Biota (ecology)">biotic</a> carbon emissions, mainly from deforestation. Banuri <i>et al.</i> (1996, p. 94)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_120-6"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-120">[120]</a></sup> calculated per capita cumulative emissions based on then-current population. The ratio in per capita emissions between industrialized countries and developing countries was estimated at more than 10 to 1.
</p><p>Including biotic emissions brings about the same controversy mentioned earlier regarding carbon sinks and land-use change (Banuri <i>et al.</i>, 1996, pp. 93–94).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-banuri_120-7"><a href="#cite_note-banuri-120">[120]</a></sup> The actual calculation of net emissions is very complex, and is affected by how carbon sinks are allocated between regions and the dynamics of the climate system.
</p><p>Non-<a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries accounted for 42% of cumulative energy-related CO<sub>2</sub> emissions between 1890 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_energy_outlook_2009_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-world_energy_outlook_2009-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>179–80</span></sup> Over this time period, the US accounted for 28% of emissions; the EU, 23%; Russia, 11%; China, 9%; other OECD countries, 5%; Japan, 4%; India, 3%; and the rest of the world, 18%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_energy_outlook_2009_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-world_energy_outlook_2009-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>179–80</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_since_a_particular_base_year">Changes since a particular base year</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Changes since a particular base year">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#Government_action_and_emissions" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol § Government action and emissions</a></div>
<p>Between 1970 and 2004, global growth in annual CO<sub>2</sub> emissions was driven by North America, Asia, and the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>  The sharp acceleration in CO<sub>2</sub> emissions since 2000 to more than a 3% increase per year (more than 2 ppm per year) from 1.1% per year during the 1990s is attributable to the lapse of formerly declining trends in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_intensity" title="Carbon intensity">carbon intensity</a> of both developing and developed nations. China was responsible for most of global growth in emissions during this period. Localised plummeting emissions associated with the collapse of the Soviet Union have been followed by slow emissions growth in this region due to more <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">efficient energy use</a>, made necessary by the increasing proportion of it that is exported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raupach_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raupach-89">[89]</a></sup> In comparison, methane has not increased appreciably, and <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> by 0.25% y<sup>−1</sup>.
</p><p>Using different base years for measuring emissions has an effect on estimates of national contributions to global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>17–18</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> This can be calculated by dividing a country's highest contribution to global warming starting from a particular base year, by that country's minimum contribution to global warming starting from a particular base year. Choosing between different base years of 1750, 1900, 1950, and 1990 has a significant effect for most countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-hohne_2010_regional_contribution_to_global_warming-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>17–18</span></sup> Within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G8" title="G8">G8</a> group of countries, it is most significant for the UK, France and Germany. These countries have a long history of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions (see the section on <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Cumulative_and_historical_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas">Cumulative and historical emissions</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Annual_emissions">Annual emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Annual emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GHG_per_capita_2000.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="940" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/GHG_per_capita_2000.svg/180px-GHG_per_capita_2000.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/GHG_per_capita_2000.svg/270px-GHG_per_capita_2000.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/GHG_per_capita_2000.svg/360px-GHG_per_capita_2000.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GHG_per_capita_2000.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Per capita anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions by country for the year 2000 including land-use change.</div></div></div>
<p>Annual per capita emissions in the industrialized countries are typically as much as ten times the average in developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grubb_kyoto_protocol_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-grubb_kyoto_protocol-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>144</span></sup> Due to China's fast economic development, its annual per capita emissions are quickly approaching the levels of those in the <a href='/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol#2012_emission_targets_and_"flexible_mechanisms"' title="Kyoto Protocol">Annex I group</a> of the Kyoto Protocol (i.e., the developed countries excluding the US).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008-132">[132]</a></sup> Other countries with fast growing emissions are <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, Iran, and Australia (which apart from the oil rich Persian Gulf states, now has the highest percapita emission rate in the world). On the other hand, annual per capita emissions of the EU-15 and the US are gradually decreasing over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008-132">[132]</a></sup> Emissions in Russia and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> have decreased fastest since 1990 due to economic restructuring in these countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Energy statistics for fast growing economies are less accurate than those for the industrialized countries. For China's annual emissions in 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Environmental_Assessment_Agency" title="Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency">Netherlands Environmental Assessment Agency</a> estimated an uncertainty range of about 10%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-pbl_annual_emissions_in_2008-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>The  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GHG_footprint" title="GHG footprint">greenhouse gas footprint</a> refers to the emissions resulting from the creation of products or services. It is more comprehensive than the commonly used <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a>, which measures only carbon dioxide, one of many greenhouse gases.
</p><p>2015 was the first year to see both total global economic growth and a reduction of carbon emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Top_emitter_countries">Top emitter countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Top emitter countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="540" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg/220px-CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg/330px-CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg/440px-CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CO2_emission_pie_chart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions">carbon dioxide emissions</a> by country.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ghg-co2-2012.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="680" height="463" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ghg-co2-2012.svg/350px-Ghg-co2-2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ghg-co2-2012.svg/525px-Ghg-co2-2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Ghg-co2-2012.svg/700px-Ghg-co2-2012.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ghg-co2-2012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The top 40 countries emitting all greenhouse gases, showing both that derived from all sources including land clearance and forestry and also the CO2 component excluding those sources. Per capita figures are included. <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.wri.org/resources/data-sets/cait-historical-emissions-data-countries-us-states-unfccc" rel="nofollow">"World Resources Institute data"</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Annual">Annual</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Annual">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2009, the annual top ten emitting countries accounted for about two-thirds of the world's annual energy-related CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Embedded_emissions">Embedded emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Embedded emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One way of attributing greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions is to measure the <a href="/wiki/Embedded_emissions" title="Embedded emissions">embedded emissions</a> (also referred to as "embodied emissions") of goods that are being consumed. Emissions are usually measured according to production, rather than consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> For example, in the main international <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaty</a> on climate change (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNFCCC" title="UNFCCC">UNFCCC</a>), countries report on emissions produced within their borders, e.g., the emissions produced from burning fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-world_energy_outlook_2009_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-world_energy_outlook_2009-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>179</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_consumption_emissions_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-davis_consumption_emissions-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>1</span></sup> Under a production-based accounting of emissions, embedded emissions on imported goods are attributed to the exporting, rather than the importing, country. Under a consumption-based accounting of emissions, embedded emissions on imported goods are attributed to the importing country, rather than the exporting, country.
</p><p>Davis and Caldeira (2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_consumption_emissions_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-davis_consumption_emissions-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>4</span></sup> found that a substantial proportion of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions are traded internationally. The net effect of trade was to export emissions from China and other emerging markets to consumers in the US, Japan, and Western Europe. Based on annual emissions data from the year 2004, and on a per-capita consumption basis, the top-5 emitting countries were found to be (in tCO<sub>2</sub> per person, per year): Luxembourg (34.7), the US (22.0), Singapore (20.2), Australia (16.7), and Canada (16.6).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davis_consumption_emissions_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-davis_consumption_emissions-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5</span></sup> Carbon Trust research revealed that approximately 25% of all CO<sub>2</sub> emissions from human activities 'flow' (i.e., are imported or exported) from one country to another. Major developed economies were found to be typically net importers of embodied carbon emissions—with UK consumption emissions 34% higher than production emissions, and Germany (29%), Japan (19%) and the US (13%) also significant net importers of embodied emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_of_policy">Effect of policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Effect of policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol_and_government_action" title="Kyoto Protocol and government action">Kyoto Protocol and government action</a></div>
<p>Governments have taken action to reduce greenhouse gas emissions (<a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">climate change mitigation</a>). Assessments of policy effectiveness have included work by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Policies implemented by governments have included<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-145">[145]</a></sup> national and regional targets to reduce emissions, promoting <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a>, and support for <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> such as Solar energy as an effective use of renewable energy because solar uses energy from the sun and does not release pollutants into the air.
</p><p>Countries and regions listed in Annex I of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Framework_Convention_on_Climate_Change" title="United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change">United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC) (i.e., the OECD and former planned economies of the Soviet Union) are required to submit periodic assessments to the UNFCCC of actions they are taking to address climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup> Analysis by the UNFCCC (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>8</span></sup> suggested that policies and measures undertaken by Annex I Parties may have produced emission savings of 1.5 thousand <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(mass)" title="Orders of magnitude (mass)">Tg</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO<sub>2</sub>-eq</a> in the year 2010, with most savings made in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_sector" title="Energy sector">energy sector</a>. The projected emissions saving of 1.5 thousand Tg CO<sub>2</sub>-eq is measured against a hypothetical "<a href="/wiki/Economics_of_climate_change_mitigation#Baselines" title="Economics of climate change mitigation">baseline</a>" of Annex I emissions, i.e., projected Annex I emissions in the absence of policies and measures. The total projected Annex I saving of 1.5 thousand CO<sub>2</sub>-eq does not include emissions savings in seven of the Annex I Parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications_145-3"><a href="#cite_note-2011_unfccc_synthesis_of_annex_I_communications-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>8</span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projections">Projections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Projections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_scenario#Quantitative_emissions_projections" title="Climate change scenario">climate change scenario § Quantitative emissions projections</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_model#Projections_of_future_climate_change" title="Global climate model">Global climate model § Projections of future climate change</a></div>
<p>A wide range of projections of future emissions have been produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Rogner <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> assessed the scientific literature on greenhouse gas projections. Rogner <i>et al.</i> (2007)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-113">[113]</a></sup> concluded that unless energy policies changed substantially, the world would continue to depend on fossil fuels until 2025–2030. Projections suggest that more than 80% of the world's energy will come from fossil fuels. This conclusion was based on "much evidence" and "high agreement" in the literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-113">[113]</a></sup> Projected annual energy-related CO<sub>2</sub> emissions in 2030 were 40–110% higher than in 2000, with two-thirds of the increase originating in developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_113-3"><a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-113">[113]</a></sup> Projected annual per capita emissions in developed country regions remained substantially lower (2.8–5.1 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> CO<sub>2</sub>) than those in developed country regions (9.6–15.1 tonnes CO<sub>2</sub>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Projections consistently showed increase in annual world emissions of <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">"Kyoto"</a> gases,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> measured in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">CO<sub>2</sub>-equivalent</a>) of 25–90% by 2030, compared to 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends_113-4"><a href="#cite_note-rogner_summary_of_emission_trends-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relative_CO2_emission_from_various_fuels">Relative CO<sub>2</sub> emission from various fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Relative CO2 emission from various fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One liter of gasoline, when used as a fuel, produces <span class="nowrap">2.32 kg</span> (about 1300 liters or 1.3 cubic meters) of carbon dioxide, a greenhouse gas. One US gallon produces 19.4 lb (1,291.5 gallons or 172.65 cubic feet)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources">Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span id="Removal_from_the_atmosphere_.28.22sinks.22.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Removal_from_the_atmosphere_("sinks")'>Removal from the atmosphere ("sinks")</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title='Edit section: Removal from the atmosphere ("sinks")'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_processes">Natural processes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Natural processes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Greenhouse gases can be <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">removed from the atmosphere</a> by various processes, as a consequence of:
</p>
<ul><li>a physical change (condensation and precipitation remove water vapor from the atmosphere).</li>
<li>a chemical reaction within the atmosphere. For example, methane is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidized" title="Oxidized">oxidized</a> by reaction with naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">hydroxyl radical</a>, OH<b>·</b> and degraded to CO<sub>2</sub> and water vapor (CO<sub>2</sub> from the oxidation of methane is not included in the methane <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">Global warming potential</a>). Other chemical reactions include solution and solid phase chemistry occurring in atmospheric aerosols.</li>
<li>a physical exchange between the atmosphere and the other compartments of the planet. An example is the mixing of atmospheric gases into the oceans.</li>
<li>a chemical change at the interface between the atmosphere and the other compartments of the planet. This is the case for CO<sub>2</sub>, which is reduced by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> of plants, and which, after dissolving in the oceans, reacts to form <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> ions (see <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a>).</li>
<li>a <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">photochemical change</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halocarbons" title="Halocarbons">Halocarbons</a> are dissociated by <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV</a> light releasing Cl<b>·</b> and F<b>·</b> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radicals</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a> with harmful effects on <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> (halocarbons are generally too stable to disappear by chemical reaction in the atmosphere).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Negative_emissions">Negative emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Negative emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_carbon_dioxide_emission" title="Negative carbon dioxide emission">Negative carbon dioxide emission</a></div>
<p>A number of technologies remove greenhouse gases emissions from the atmosphere. Most widely analysed are those that remove carbon dioxide from the atmosphere, either to geologic formations such as <a href="/wiki/Bio-energy_with_carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Bio-energy with carbon capture and storage">bio-energy with carbon capture and storage</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoyalSociety_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-RoyalSociety-157">[157]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_air_capture" title="Carbon dioxide air capture">carbon dioxide air capture</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoyalSociety_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-RoyalSociety-157">[157]</a></sup> or to the soil as in the case with <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">biochar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoyalSociety_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-RoyalSociety-157">[157]</a></sup> The IPCC has pointed out that many long-term climate scenario models require large-scale manmade negative emissions to avoid serious climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC2007_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC2007-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_scientific_research">History of scientific research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: History of scientific research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the late 19th century scientists experimentally discovered that <span class="chemf nowrap">N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> do not absorb infrared radiation (called, at that time, "dark radiation"), while water (both as true vapor and condensed in the form of microscopic droplets suspended in clouds) and CO<sub>2</sub> and other poly-atomic gaseous molecules do absorb infrared radiation. In the early 20th century researchers realized that greenhouse gases in the atmosphere made Earth's overall temperature higher than it would be without them. During the late 20th century, a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Scientific opinion on climate change">scientific consensus</a> evolved that increasing concentrations of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere cause a substantial rise in global temperatures and changes to other parts of the climate system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">consequences</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Physical_impacts_of_climate_change" title="Physical impacts of climate change">environment</a> and for <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming_on_human_health" title="Effects of global warming on human health">human health</a>.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide_emissions">Carbon dioxide emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Greenhouse_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Carbon dioxide emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20081224193511/http://www.eia.doe.gov/emeu/iea/res.html" rel="nofollow">International Energy Annual: Reserves</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.cmdl.noaa.gov/ccgg/trends/" rel="nofollow">Trends in Atmospheric Carbon Dioxide</a> at NOAA</li>

<li><a class="external text" href="http://cdiac.ornl.gov/" rel="nofollow">Carbon Dioxide Information Analysis Center</a> (CDIAC)</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://siteresources.worldbank.org/INTDATASTA/64199955-1178226923002/21322619/LGDB2007.pdf" rel="nofollow">Little Green Data Book 2007</a>, World Bank. Lists CO<sub>2</sub> statistics by country, including per capita and by country income class.</li>
